  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_492342e3-2ef9-4cea-b269-c0a42608e11d'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<span id="freeTextContainer9174788593035446650">Enola's landlady, Mrs. Tupper, is the closest thing Enola has to family these days, besides her occasional run-ins with her brother Sherlock. Even though Mrs. Tupper is nearly deaf and can barely cook, she's an endearing presence as Enola longs for her absent mother. So imagine her horror when Enola comes home to find Mrs. Tupper kidnapped! Who would take her, and why? And</span>
  <span id="freeText9174788593035446650" style="display:none">Enola's landlady, Mrs. Tupper, is the closest thing Enola has to family these days, besides her occasional run-ins with her brother Sherlock. Even though Mrs. Tupper is nearly deaf and can barely cook, she's an endearing presence as Enola longs for her absent mother. So imagine her horror when Enola comes home to find Mrs. Tupper kidnapped! Who would take her, and why? And what does Florence Nightingale have to do with it? From the master of mystery Nancy Springer, here's another absorbing adventure for our award-winning, unstoppable heroine, Enola Holmes.</span>

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<div class='spoiler'>
This question contains spoilers…
<span class='spoilerAnswerText'>
<a href="/questions/530810-in-the-epilogue-it-is-mentioned-briefly"><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[In the epilogue, it is mentioned, briefly, that Mrs. Tupper has regained her sense of hearing. This is not explained, so I assume it relies on some known medical innovation of the period, but I cannot find any better particulars. Does anyone know -how- exactly Mrs. Tupper's deafness might have been treated? <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></a>
</span>
</div>

<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/35809089-erika"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1473156640i/35809089._UX100_CR0,1,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/35809089-erika">Erika</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<div class='spoiler'>
This answer contains spoilers…
<span class='spoilerAnswerText'>
<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  
    She is still deaf. Beethoven was famously deaf himself, and many (if not all) of his pieces use tones that can be “heard” through the vibrations creat<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">…more</a>
    She is still deaf. Beethoven was famously deaf himself, and many (if not all) of his pieces use tones that can be “heard” through the vibrations created by the music. Mrs Tupper, sitting next to the piano, can therefore “hear” the music that is being played on the instrument.<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">(less)</a>
  
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>
</span>

It was very fast. But still it was great! Sad to say there is only 1 book left! Good thing I can hopefully look forwards to more Enola Holmes movies :)</span>

  <span id="freeText16214619718882037917" style="display:none">Watch the review on <a href="https://youtu.be/aGniRNhULIU" rel="nofollow noopener">Youtube</a><br />Hear the review on my <a href="https://anchor.fm/digitalamrit/episodes/Review-of-the-Enola-Holmes-series-by-Nancy-Springer-elgs9j" rel="nofollow noopener">Podcast</a><br />Read the full review at my blog <a href="https://www.digitalamrit.com/post/review-of-the-enola-holmes-series-by-nancy-springer" rel="nofollow noopener">Digital Amrit</a><br /><br />tl;dr: The ‘Enola Holmes’ books are an engaging, entertaining and empowering take on the Holmes mythos which will be a hit with girls in their tweens.<br /><br />This series is a set of six short books set from the perspective of Sherlock Holmes’s younger sister, Enola Holmes. The series starts with their mother disappearing on Enola’s fourteenth birthday. Mycroft and Sherlock are not interested in pursuing their mother’s disappearance due to an old family quarrel and want to put Enola in boarding school. Enola has been bought up by her mother to be a free and independent thinker. She refuses to be part of the patriarchal and misogynistic system of the 1900s, runs away to London and tries to find her mother on her own. Her struggles to survive in London; her attempts to help others by using her powers of intelligence and deduction and her constant brushes with her brothers form the stories of the rest of the series.<br /><br />Each of the books has a central mystery at its core that Enola has to solve. Typically, these are focused on women and their problems in the era. <div id="review_3600832517" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3600832517" />
    <a title="Annette" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11358898-annette"><img alt="Annette" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1475048089p2/11358898.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3600832517?book_show_action=true">Nov 08, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Annette" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Annette" href="/user/show/11358898-annette">Annette</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/55340478-the-case-of-the-cryptic-crinoline">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3600832517" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17341058268684174468">Enola Holmes has certainly become one of my favorite characters ever. And maybe the book version even more than the movie version. (I think adding a love interest and possible love story to the movie may have ruined the Enola Holmes as she is in the books...) So, no matter what happens and no matter how many other books I have to read, I will make time every weekend for an Enola Holmes novel. (Well, I'll finish the last one next week...)<br/><br/>And this one was once again a truly nice story! The entire </span>
  <span id="freeText17341058268684174468" style="display:none">Enola Holmes has certainly become one of my favorite characters ever. And maybe the book version even more than the movie version. (I think adding a love interest and possible love story to the movie may have ruined the Enola Holmes as she is in the books...) So, no matter what happens and no matter how many other books I have to read, I will make time every weekend for an Enola Holmes novel. (Well, I'll finish the last one next week...)<br /><br />And this one was once again a truly nice story! The entire plot focusses on the case, mostly because this time the case hits very close to home, maybe even closer than the Watson case a few books back did. Enola's emotional investment really makes this case different from the other cases. She starts to make mistakes and she fails to make connections and in a way that's also really nice. It makes her human and very relatable. Especially because for once as a reader we can make some connections before Enola does.<br /><br />Once more this book also shows ugly parts of society and especially the pressure it put on ladies. Although I don't really like the trope of invalids "faking it", I really understand where it comes from in this book and also why the character involved deemed it necessary. It says a lot about society, about the pressure on girls and the lengths they had to go to follow their dreams instead of being molded into perfect wives and mothers. <br /><br />However, this case once more helped Sherlock Holmes to understand that what he thinks is best for his sister, might not actually be in her best interest. It took him five books to finally start to understand it, but we're getting there. And that's good for Enola, because can you imagine that a brilliant, free, creative and self reliant soul like Enola would not be able and allowed to use her talents anymore? Which, undoubtedly, was the story of a lot of girls in this time in history. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17341058268684174468" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_273655434" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/273655434" />
    <a title="Ellen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6006611-ellen"><img alt="Ellen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1314715818p2/6006611.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/273655434?book_show_action=true">Feb 07, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ellen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ellen" href="/user/show/6006611-ellen">Ellen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer273655434" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5847220822069382172">This Enola Holmes mystery brings Enola's landlady, Mrs. Tupper, to life. Instead of just a deaf old lady who cooks cheap but creative meals, she becomes a real person with a history of a husband who died young, a miscarried baby, a sense of style, and memories of being nursed by Florence Nightingale during the Crimean War. In this book, Florence herself explains to Sherlock why his misguided desire to put his sister into a boarding school is incentive enough to keep Enola on the run from him. He</span>
  <span id="freeText5847220822069382172" style="display:none">This Enola Holmes mystery brings Enola's landlady, Mrs. Tupper, to life. Instead of just a deaf old lady who cooks cheap but creative meals, she becomes a real person with a history of a husband who died young, a miscarried baby, a sense of style, and memories of being nursed by Florence Nightingale during the Crimean War. In this book, Florence herself explains to Sherlock why his misguided desire to put his sister into a boarding school is incentive enough to keep Enola on the run from him. He almost seems to understand, and his respect for Enola's independence and intelligence grows.<br /><br />Nancy Springer's descriptions of London and the conditions during the Crimean War make the filth and disease very real, and they help me to appreciate the cleanliness and ease that I now enjoy. But those descriptions are just the background to an intriguing mystery that keeps me reading from page to page and chapter to chapter until I finally finish the book with a great feeling of satisfaction that Enola has triumphed once again.<br /><br />Besides learning a bit about life over 100 years ago, I also increase my vocabulary with fun and interesting words. It helps to have a dictionary handy, although you don't have to in order to follow the story line. I learned that a misogynist--which is one of the words that Florence uses to describe Sherlock's and Enola's older brother, Mycroft--is a woman hater. How's that for a new word to add to my vocabulary!</span>
  <a data-text-id="5847220822069382172" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_166923726" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/166923726" />
    <a title="Ann" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/446509-ann"><img alt="Ann" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1194659951p2/446509.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/166923726?book_show_action=true">May 09, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ann" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ann" href="/user/show/446509-ann">Ann</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/446509-ann?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/446509-ann?shelf=audio-books">audio-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/446509-ann?shelf=mysteries">mysteries</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer166923726" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14155255219338392323">3.5 stars <br/>Audio version - narrated by the incredible Katherine Kellgren!!!<br/><br/>In this, the fifth installment in the Enola Holmes mystery series, Enola must help solve a mystery entangling her dear old landlady, Mrs. Tuppper. And the start of the mystery dates all the way back, some several decades prior, to a secret message that the Mrs. Tupper was supposedly meant to be carrier for. But poor Mrs. Tupper doesn't seem to know/recall a single thing about it - but there are those who assume otherwise..</span>
  <span id="freeText14155255219338392323" style="display:none">3.5 stars <br />Audio version - narrated by the incredible Katherine Kellgren!!!<br /><br />In this, the fifth installment in the Enola Holmes mystery series, Enola must help solve a mystery entangling her dear old landlady, Mrs. Tuppper. And the start of the mystery dates all the way back, some several decades prior, to a secret message that the Mrs. Tupper was supposedly meant to be carrier for. But poor Mrs. Tupper doesn't seem to know/recall a single thing about it - but there are those who assume otherwise...<br /><br />I see that I am in a huge minority here in my three star rating - and perhaps my lack of involvement/investment was because, in listening to the audio book, I was more apt to be distracted (not by any fault of the narration or story, but because I'd be working and listening at the same time). Or perhaps it was because I've been listening to so many Enola books lately and perhaps I need more time in between.<br />I just felt this story didn't really have the same detail as the previous books and while the overall story (series-wise) is advanced by the end, it seemed, to me, not as epic as it should have.<br /><br />I also would like to see Springer look into other areas of growth for both Enola and her story lines - I find Enola's issues with her Mum haven't really grown much in the last three books, and I feel the stories would benefit from more diversity than relying so much on the down-trodden-ness of women in that era (thankfully this book dealt less with this than other books). I think it's a great issue to bring to light, and I think it's interesting that Enola, with her insight into women's lives, can solve mysteries that her brother, Sherlock Holmes, cannot - but I do think sometimes Springer makes too broad of statements regarding women of that era. Also, I'd like to see a little more depth from the supporting characters (the ones that are specific to one mystery, I mean. Those that appear in multiple books are nicely drawn).<br /><br />These books seem to be geared for "middle grade" ages 9 or 10 and up. I would hesitate to suggest them for such a young audience. Certainly there's much of the books that are perfectly fine, but this book alone provoked more difficult and grim images in the first chapter alone than the entirety of the fourth book (which, the author does state that the delicate reader may skip the prologue or first chapter - whichever it is - and go right to the story, which if one does then the rest of the book isn't nearly as vivid as that initial war infirmary scene).<br /><br />All this said, I do really like the books - and I do really like Enola. I will definitely be finishing out the series! I like that its a mystery for those that might not be ready for, say, Agatha Cristie. I like that you can solve the puzzles along with Enola (something I would have probably attempted when I was younger!) and I was surprised by quite a few turns in this book, and found myself wondering why I hadn't "seen that coming." I would recommend these to other readers/listeners (just not until they are Young Adult readers).</span>
  <a data-text-id="14155255219338392323" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<span id="freeTextContainer6871151175289247476">The penultimate book in the series tones it down a bit, cutting back on the swinging from the roof fittings and other Errol Flynn style shenanigans. Instead Enola relies on her usual considerable talent for disguise, sketch making, cryptology and of course her endless supply of pluck.<br/>In her hunt to recover her kidnapped landlady she is forced to consult Florence Nightingale, which, teachers take note, means the Crimean War is a part of the story.   <span id="freeText6871151175289247476" style="display:none">The penultimate book in the series tones it down a bit, cutting back on the swinging from the roof fittings and other Errol Flynn style shenanigans. Instead Enola relies on her usual considerable talent for disguise, sketch making, cryptology and of course her endless supply of pluck.<br />In her hunt to recover her kidnapped landlady she is forced to consult Florence Nightingale, which, teachers take note, means the Crimean War is a part of the story. This series is fun for kids and if the spark of interest in history is fanned then so much the better. Barring casting Florence as a spy the author respects the historical accuracy of the historical figure. <br />One of the better additions to the series.</span>

<div id="review_2988604888" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2988604888" />
    <a title="Paul Weiss" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/51664350-paul-weiss"><img alt="Paul Weiss" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1453113069p2/51664350.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2988604888?book_show_action=true">Sep 24, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul Weiss" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul Weiss" href="/user/show/51664350-paul-weiss">Paul Weiss</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51664350-paul-weiss?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51664350-paul-weiss?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51664350-paul-weiss?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2988604888" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4972895483948943163"><b>Enola goes five for five as a successful "perditorian"!</b><br/><br/>Pitched at a reading level considerably higher than the justifiably famous Nancy Drew series, <b>THE CASE OF THE CRYPTIC CRINOLINE</b> continues the adventures of Enola Holmes, the hitherto unknown, late-arriving younger sister of Mycroft and Sherlock Holmes, who was introduced so capably in <b>THE CASE OF THE MISSING MARQUESS</b>. <br/><br/>A budding young feminist, intelligent far beyond her tender age of only fourteen years, Enola Holmes is living incognito in L</span>
  <span id="freeText4972895483948943163" style="display:none"><b>Enola goes five for five as a successful "perditorian"!</b><br /><br />Pitched at a reading level considerably higher than the justifiably famous Nancy Drew series, <b>THE CASE OF THE CRYPTIC CRINOLINE</b> continues the adventures of Enola Holmes, the hitherto unknown, late-arriving younger sister of Mycroft and Sherlock Holmes, who was introduced so capably in <b>THE CASE OF THE MISSING MARQUESS</b>. <br /><br />A budding young feminist, intelligent far beyond her tender age of only fourteen years, Enola Holmes is living incognito in London, hiding from her brilliant brothers for fear that they would force her into the stultifying life of a Victorian boarding school for young ladies. Sherlock and Mycroft are portrayed as typical 19th century men in their attitude toward women and whatever intellect they may possess. That is to say, they are at least patronizing and chauvinist and perhaps, in Mycroft's case, downright misogynist. <br /><br />Despite being of independent means with the financial resources that her mother provided in <b>THE CASE OF THE MISSING MARQUESS</b>, Enola has decided that she will live her life as a <i>"perditorian"</i>, a "finder of lost things". In <b>THE CASE OF THE CRYPTIC CRINOLINE</b>, Enola tackles the disappearance of her own sweet landlady, the very deaf Mrs Tupper, when she is kidnapped after what modern readers would term a house invasion. The clues are sparse - a story about Mrs Tupper's history as a broken-hearted widow in the Crimean War and some old embroidery on a crinoline. But Enola is stubbornly determined to find her and repay the kindness that Mrs Tupper has shown her by acting, in effect, as a loving, thoughtful stand-in for the real mother that Enola hasn't seen since she left home so many months earlier. Of course, her sleuthing constantly places her in danger of being shipped off to that ever so repugnant boarding school for young ladies should her brothers, Sherlock and Mycroft, ever manage to penetrate her numerous disguises and lay hands on her.<br /><br />Nancy Springer's wonderfully innovative and imaginative series is now maturing as it reaches its fifth title but it certainly isn't showing any signs of staleness or tiring. The portrayal of Crimean heroine and foresighted developer of the modern nursing profession, Florence Nightingale, adds a heightened verve and sense of possible reality to this mystery. Clearly the ideas, the atmosphere, the characterization and the plotting continue to flow and I'll be looking forward to the sixth title, <b>THE CASE OF THE GYPSY GOODBYE</b>. Highly recommended.<br /><br />Paul Weiss</span>
  <a data-text-id="4972895483948943163" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer9700468047234125173">Reread in September 2020. <br/>Content warning for description of the atrocities of war, slur used to describe the Romani people.<br/><br/>What a good book! It really did make me want to read about Florence Nightingale. Enola investigating her housekeeper's disappearance was also quite sweet. One more book!<br/><br/><br/><br/><br/><br/><br/>-- Original review<br/>This book is of such superior quality it's a wonder Nancy Springer isn't huge spot-her-in-the-street-and-beg-for-autograph kind of huge.   <span id="freeText9700468047234125173" style="display:none">Reread in September 2020. <br />Content warning for description of the atrocities of war, slur used to describe the Romani people.<br /><br />What a good book! It really did make me want to read about Florence Nightingale. Enola investigating her housekeeper's disappearance was also quite sweet. One more book!<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br />-- Original review<br />This book is of such superior quality it's a wonder Nancy Springer isn't huge spot-her-in-the-street-and-beg-for-autograph kind of huge. Enola's path this time crosses that of Florence Nightingale in a gripping mystery that takes us back to the Crimean War. I love when Enola's story intertwines with that of famous historical figures. The description Springer makes of war is absolutely chilling and well worth a read. I also really enjoyed her eye for detail - it's lovely to be able to decode a message alongside Enola. She doesn't talk down to her readers, and I found that refreshing.<br />Enola still is the wonderful girl she's been from day one and it's a privilege to follow her for her social conscience is truly welcome.</span>

<div id="review_1071109525" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1071109525" />
    <a title="⋟Kiki⋞" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3555205-kiki"><img alt="⋟Kiki⋞" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1390274805p2/3555205.jpg" /></a>

<div id="review_1091204027" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1091204027" />
    <a title="Terri M." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1127354-terri-m"><img alt="Terri M." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598971206p2/1127354.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1091204027?book_show_action=true">Oct 28, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Terri M." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Terri M." href="/user/show/1127354-terri-m">Terri M.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1127354-terri-m?shelf=action-adventure">action-adventure</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1127354-terri-m?shelf=books-i-own">books-i-own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1127354-terri-m?shelf=format-audiobook">format-audiobook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1127354-terri-m?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1127354-terri-m?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1127354-terri-m?shelf=reading-lvl-young-adult">reading-lvl-young-adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1091204027" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6706621829221082597">I was pleasantly surprised by <em>The Case of the Cryptic Crinoline</em> by Nancy Springer. I took a chance and downloaded it via the SYNC audiobook program this past summer. I seem to be game for new takes on known franchises. I didn't even realize that this novel was a part of a series!<br/><br/>I guess that's a good a place to start as any! You need not to have read/listened to any of the preceding novels to understand what is happening in <em>The Case of the Cryptic Crinoline</em>. Enough information is given, when nee</span>
  <span id="freeText6706621829221082597" style="display:none">I was pleasantly surprised by <em>The Case of the Cryptic Crinoline</em> by Nancy Springer. I took a chance and downloaded it via the SYNC audiobook program this past summer. I seem to be game for new takes on known franchises. I didn't even realize that this novel was a part of a series!<br /><br />I guess that's a good a place to start as any! You need not to have read/listened to any of the preceding novels to understand what is happening in <em>The Case of the Cryptic Crinoline</em>. Enough information is given, when needed, to help you understand why Enola is on her own and why she is at odds with her brother, Sherlock.<br /><br />I loved that this story involved some real historical figures. That's my favorite thing about historical fiction—when it brings in elements of the real word. <br /><br />I did love the narrator, Katherine Kellgren. She did a marvelous job with the characters' voices especially Mrs. Tupper. I snorted with delight whenever a conversation was started with the deaf landlady. It reminded me of conversations with my own grandmother. It was a delight to listen to.<br /><br />My favorite thing about the book? Enola avoiding her brother. It's not often someone gets the break ahead of Holmes, but Enola's drive to solve the crime on her own, before her brother, adds an exciting element to the plot.<br /><br />I am giving <em>The Case of the Cryptic Crinoline</em> by Nancy Springer a thumbs up. It was a delightful surprise and I enjoyed running around London with Ms. Enola Holmes.<br /><br />BONUS POINTS! I didn't feel it was necessary to have read (or listened) to the previous books in the series to understand Enola's backstory. Enough hits were given throughout the story to explain why she avoiding Holmes and the on-going mystery of their mother.<br /><br /><br /><a href="http://www.secondrunreviews.com/2015/02/book-review-cryptic-crinoline-nancy-springer.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">This review was originally posted on Second Run Reviews</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6706621829221082597" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer149833502" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16982409201446041633">This, the fifth in the Enola Holmes series, has Enola tracking down her beloved landlady, deaf-as-a-post Mrs. Tupper. One day, after arriving home from her job as Dr. Ragostin's secretary, Enola is asked by Mrs. Tupper to help figure out a message that is sent to her regarding "information from the Bird". Mrs. Tupper has no idea what this is in reference too and is a bit frightened at the underlying threat if the "information" is not forthcoming. And with good reason, for the very next day, Mrs.</span>
  <span id="freeText16982409201446041633" style="display:none">This, the fifth in the Enola Holmes series, has Enola tracking down her beloved landlady, deaf-as-a-post Mrs. Tupper. One day, after arriving home from her job as Dr. Ragostin's secretary, Enola is asked by Mrs. Tupper to help figure out a message that is sent to her regarding "information from the Bird". Mrs. Tupper has no idea what this is in reference too and is a bit frightened at the underlying threat if the "information" is not forthcoming. And with good reason, for the very next day, Mrs. Tupper is kidnapped from her home.<br /><br />Enola manages to find a few clues as she searches through the ransacked home which eventually lead her to the bedside of Miss Florence Nightengale, "The Lady of the Lamp", the famous nurse and activist of the Crimean War. As it turns out, Mrs. Tupper was there in Scutari with her husband, as purveyors of goods that were sold to the army and camp followers on the Crimean penninsula. And that is where Mr. Tupper died, due to disease and neglect by the army doctors and orderlies. Miss Nightengale somehow managed to get Mrs. Tupper home to England, even providing her a dress and money to get there. Poor Mrs. Tupper, already deaf due to cannon-fire, arrive safely in England and proceeds to get her life back in order but never forgets Miss Nightengale, even saving the dress she was given. Thirty years later, this dress turns out to be the key to getting Mrs. Tupper home safely.<br /><br />Very creative story in this one, loved meeting Florence Nightengale and reading more about the women suffragists movement. Enola also picks up news of her mother though is no closer to actually finding her.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16982409201446041633" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer9780376608278706494">I've been wanting to read the <i>Enola Holmes Mysteries</i> for quite a while now, so when I saw the audiobook of the <i>The Cryptic Crinoline</i> was made available for free through YA Sync I downloaded it immediately.<br/><br/>Enola Holmes is 14 years old, and that means she is 20 years younger than her brother Sherlock and 27 years younger than Mycroft. After her mother went missing, Enola's famous brothers have attempted to send her to boarding school, but in order to avoid this she went into hiding in London, and </span>
  <span id="freeText9780376608278706494" style="display:none">I've been wanting to read the <i>Enola Holmes Mysteries</i> for quite a while now, so when I saw the audiobook of the <i>The Cryptic Crinoline</i> was made available for free through YA Sync I downloaded it immediately.<br><br>Enola Holmes is 14 years old, and that means she is 20 years younger than her brother Sherlock and 27 years younger than Mycroft. After her mother went missing, Enola's famous brothers have attempted to send her to boarding school, but in order to avoid this she went into hiding in London, and solves cases on the side. In his particular installment, Mrs. Tupper, her landlady is kidnapped and somehow Florence Nightingale is involved.<br><br>I really liked this homage to Conan Doyle's stories - we even get to see the great detective and his biographer! Enola is a very likeable, clever, and resourceful character. She loves her brothers but is afraid for her future if they manage to succeed in relieving her of her freedom, even if they believe it's the right thing to do she must disagree. I like that Springer has broached the topic of Holmes' views on women in direct comparison to Enola and what she learns of the women's suffrage movement. <br><br>I will definitely be returning to this series revolving around this delightfully brilliant, independent,and courageous heroine!<br><br>In my mind, I saw (and heard) Jeremy Brett's portrayal of Sherlock Holmes while reading <i>The Case of the Cryptic Crinoline</i>. <br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1403681806ra/10128339.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer42482026" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4409226675872189076">Ahh Enola, I am so happy to have another book featuring Sherlock Holme's quirky younger sister. The Enola Holmes books are far from perfect but the characters are so engaging it's hard not to love them. This is another fun addition to the series. Once again Enola must solve a mystery, this one involving her sweet, old and very deaf land-lady. The plot jumps around a bit and I am forced to marvel at the idiocy of villians in Enola's world yet again. The historical facts about Florence Nightingale</span>
  <span id="freeText4409226675872189076" style="display:none">Ahh Enola, I am so happy to have another book featuring Sherlock Holme's quirky younger sister. The Enola Holmes books are far from perfect but the characters are so engaging it's hard not to love them. This is another fun addition to the series. Once again Enola must solve a mystery, this one involving her sweet, old and very deaf land-lady. The plot jumps around a bit and I am forced to marvel at the idiocy of villians in Enola's world yet again. The historical facts about Florence Nightingale that are woven into the narrative are a fun bonus. The entire basis of the mystery seems very shaky and improbable to me and doesn't bear close examination. Also, the morse code message Enola sends in one portion of the book is wrong - according to the morse code cheat sheet at the end of the book she sent TOT not SOS. Yes I am geeky enough I tried to decipher it before Enola revealed what the message was and therefor was quite confused. There are a dozen little nit-picky things that I could focus on that in any other book would be driving me nuts - and have me lobbing off stars. But I despite them all I really enjoyed this book. It's just fun. What can I say, in this case the characters have won me over completely. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4409226675872189076" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2070689783" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6344097157924000550">Enola finds herself in the middle of a new case when her landlady is kidnapped. Mrs. Tupper is deaf and is a horrible cook but she is the nearest thing Enola has in the way of family now that her mother has disappeared and her brothers are too eager to plan her life for her to want to have anything to do with them. <br/><br/>The seeds of this mystery were sown during the Crimean War when Mrs. Tupper was there with her husband who was selling things to the soldiers. Her husband caught one of the many illne</span>
  <span id="freeText6344097157924000550" style="display:none">Enola finds herself in the middle of a new case when her landlady is kidnapped. Mrs. Tupper is deaf and is a horrible cook but she is the nearest thing Enola has in the way of family now that her mother has disappeared and her brothers are too eager to plan her life for her to want to have anything to do with them. <br /><br />The seeds of this mystery were sown during the Crimean War when Mrs. Tupper was there with her husband who was selling things to the soldiers. Her husband caught one of the many illnesses rampant in the area and was in a hospital where Florence Nightingale was nursing. After her husband's death, Miss Nightingale arranged for Mrs. Tupper to return to England. <br /><br />Now someone is quite interested in something that Mrs. Tupper inadvertently carried back home with her. Enola has to discover what Mrs. Tupper had, contact Florence Nightingale, discover her missing landlady and stay away from her brother Sherlock who is also on the case.<br /><br />This was another excellent middle grade mystery with codes, disguises, daring deeds and all sorts of adventure set in Victorian England.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6344097157924000550" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer2420593835233091360">On her fourteenth birthday, Enola Holmes (youngest sibling of Mycroft and Sherlock) woke to find that her mother had left in the night, leaving behind only a series of cryptic clues. To avoid being sent to boarding school, Enola flees her brothers' care and heads to London, where she alternately looks for her mother and solves mysteries. She is adept at interviewing witnesses, constructing disguises, and cryptography, yet she is still just fourteen. When her deaf old landlady (who has filled a m</span>
  <span id="freeText2420593835233091360" style="display:none">On her fourteenth birthday, Enola Holmes (youngest sibling of Mycroft and Sherlock) woke to find that her mother had left in the night, leaving behind only a series of cryptic clues. To avoid being sent to boarding school, Enola flees her brothers' care and heads to London, where she alternately looks for her mother and solves mysteries. She is adept at interviewing witnesses, constructing disguises, and cryptography, yet she is still just fourteen. When her deaf old landlady (who has filled a mother's role for Enola) is kidnapped, Enola springs into action. She will stop at nothing to discover what happened to Mrs. Tupper and get her back.<br /><br />Another wonderfully engaging adventure for Enola, rife with delightful historical details (like Mrs.T's pride in her window, though it costs her window taxes) and great interactions between Enola and Sherlock Holmes.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('wb4FPuFKqgDzJZZY+dZ0z8OmGqW0MTGRMAzNlY+D6MHWnxIDU3N0uaGOTccGp7eRFo/594ixV8AZgxE2tyQA2g==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

Fauber<\/a><span title=\"Goodreads Author!\">*<\/span>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.12 avg rating &mdash; 25 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2019\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer17292896979037774539\">Today we take for granted that a telescope allows us to see galaxies millions of light years away. But before its invention, people used nothing more than their naked eye to fathom what took place in the visible sky.  So how did four men in the 1500\'<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText17292896979037774539\" style=\"display:none\">Today we take for granted that a telescope allows us to see galaxies millions of light years away. But before its invention, people used nothing more than their naked eye to fathom what took place in the visible sky.  So how did four men in the 1500\'s—of different nationality, age, religion, and class—collaborate to discover that the Earth revolved around the Sun? With this radical discovery that went against the Church, they created our contemporary world—and with it, the uneasy conditions of modern life.\n\n\nHeaven on Earth is an intimate examination of this scientific family—that of Nicolaus Copernicus, Tycho Brahe, Johannes Kepler, and Galileo Galilei. Fauber juxtaposes their scientific work with insight into their personal lives and political considerations, which shaped their pursuit of knowledge. Uniquely, he shows how their intergenerational collaboration was actually what made the scientific revolution possible.  \n\n\nRanging from the birth of astronomy and the methods of early scientific research, Fauber reveals the human story that underlies this civilization altering discovery. And, contrary to the competitive nature of research today, collaboration was key to early scientific discovery.  Before the rise of university research institutions, deep thinkers only had each other. They created a kind of family, related to each other via intellectual pursuit rather than blood.\n\n\nThese men called each other “brothers,” “fathers,” and “sons,” and laid the foundations of modern science through familial co-work. And though the sixteenth century was far from the an open society for women, There were female pioneers in this “family” as well, including Brahe\'s sister Sophie, Kepler’s mother, and Galileo\'s daughter.\n\n\nFilled with rich characters and sweeping historical scope, Heaven on Earth reveals how the strong connections between these pillars of intellectual history moved science forward—and how, without them, we might have waited a long time for a heliocentric model of the universe. <\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"17292896979037774539\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return Elizabeth agrees to help Fletcher, if only to insure her secret identity is never discovered.    For the first time, Elizabeth experiences the thrill of a cat-and-mouse adventure reminiscent of one of her own novels as she tries to throw Fletcher off her scent. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer8971144163900287997">	<br/>BIO -- NANCY SPRINGER<br/><br/><br/> Nancy Springer has passed the fifty-book milestone, having written that many novels for adults, young adults and children, in genres including mythic fantasy, contemporary fiction, magical realism, horror, and mystery -- although she did not realize she wrote mystery until she won the Edgar Allan Poe Award from the Mystery Writers of America two years in succession. DARK LIE</span>
  <span id="freeText8971144163900287997" style="display:none">	<br />BIO -- NANCY SPRINGER<br /><br /><br /> Nancy Springer has passed the fifty-book milestone, having written that many novels for adults, young adults and children, in genres including mythic fantasy, contemporary fiction, magical realism, horror, and mystery -- although she did not realize she wrote mystery until she won the Edgar Allan Poe Award from the Mystery Writers of America two years in succession. DARK LIE, recently released from NAL, is her first venture into mass-market psychological suspense. <br /> Born in Montclair, New Jersey, Nancy Springer moved with her family to Gettysburg, of Civil War fame, when she was thirteen. She spent the next forty-six years in Pennsylvania, raising two children (Jonathan, now 38, and Nora, 34), writing, horseback riding, fishing, and birdwatching. In 2007 she surprised her friends and herself by moving with her second husband to an isolated area of the Florida panhandle, where the birdwatching is spectacular and where, when fishing, she occasionally catches an alligator.<br /><br /></span>
